==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The use of Gas Foil Bearings (GFBs) has increased considerably over the past decades due
to their suitability for high-speed applications as well as research advancements leading
to performance improvements. When using air as lubricant, GFBs become a sustainable
and environmentally friendly machine element. Current challenges include fine manufac-
turing tolerances, uncertainties associated with friction between moving and stationary
parts, nonlinearities caused by fluidŌĆōstructure interactions, low load-carrying capacity,
wear during start and stop, and stability issues. A need for accurate models predicting
the static, dynamic, and thermal properties of GFBs continue to exist, while obtaining suf-
ficient model accuracy is challenged by solution times, and modelling complexity primarily
due to a) the multi-domain nature of the problem, b) the compressibility of the lubricant,
and c) the compliant foil structure composed of bump foil and top foil. Recent research
offers increasingly detailed and accurate simulation of GFBs, and cutting-edge models
predict improvement of load-carrying capacity, low-velocity operation, and stability limits
by introducing actively controlled hybridisation.
This project focuses on Hybrid Gas Foil Bearings (HGFBs) and its primary original con-
tribution is the incorporation of hybridisation driven by piezoelectric actuators into a
state-of-the-art GFB model.
A four-domain analytical model is developed and imple-
mented in an existing programme in the programming languages C and Python, and it
is verified for steady state simulation against the original, three-domain numerical model.
The transient implementation is tested using a ramp function, a step function and a sine
function, verifying the implementation. All tests are based on a rigid rotor supported by
two four-pad GFBs.
Furthermore, the project investigates top foil modelling techniques, which continue to be
a subject of dispute within the field, through both a comparison of different models as
well as stress computations for shell element models. A computationally efficient EulerŌĆō
Bernoulli beam element model is found to be a good approximation for a computationally
more expensive curved shell element model, whereas a non-curved shell element model is
found to be less accurate. This is due to the latter overestimating the dependency of the
radial foil deformation on the axial coordinate due to it not accounting for the membrane
stresses in the top foil.

Resum├® (Danish)
Gasfolielejer har vundet frem i l├Ėbet af de seneste ├źrtier som f├Ėlge af lejetypens eg-
nethed til h├Ėjhastigheds-maskineri samt pr├”stations-forbedringer drevet af forsknings-
fremskridt. N├źr atmosf├”risk luft benyttes som sm├Ėremiddel, er gasfolielejer et b├”redygtigt
og milj├Ėvenligt maskinelement.
Nuv├”rende udfordringer inkluderer fremstillingstoler-
ancer, usikkerhed forbundet med friktion mellem bev├”gelige og station├”re dele og ikke-
lineariteter for├źrsaget af interaktioner mellem sm├Ėremiddel og maskindele. Der er fortsat
et behov for n├Ėjagtige modeller, der kan forudsige gasfolielejers statiske, dynamiske, og
termiske egenskaber, mens tilstr├”kkelig modeln├Ėjagtighed udfordres af l├Ėsningstider og
modelleringskompleksitet prim├”rt p├ź grund af a) problemets multi-dom├”ne-karakt├®r, b)
sm├Ėremidlets kompressibilitet og c) den fleksible foliestruktur best├źende af b├Ėlge-folie og
top-folie. Nyere forskning tilbyder st├Ėrre detaljegrad og n├Ėjagtighed i simuleringer af gas-
folielejer, og avancerede modeller forudsiger forbedring af b├”reevne, lavhastigheds-drift og
stabilitetsgr├”nser ved introduktion af aktivt kontrolleret indspr├Ėjtning.
Dette projekt omhandler hybrid-gasfolielejer og dets vigtigste bidrag er inkorporering af
indspr├Ėjtning baseret p├ź piezoelektriske aktuatorer i en avanceret gasfolieleje-model. En
analytisk model fordelt p├ź fire dom├”ner er blevet udviklet og implementeret i programmer-
ingssprogene C og Python og er verificeret i forhold til den oprindelige numeriske model
med tre dom├”ner i steady state. Den transiente implementering testes ved hj├”lp af en
rampefunktion, en trinfunktion og en sinusfunktion, og simulationerne verificerer koden.
Alle nummeriske fors├Ėg udf├Ėres for en stiv rotor underst├Ėttet af to gasfolielejer med fire
sko hver.
Derudover unders├Ėges topfolie-modeller, hvilket fortsat er genstand for uenighed inden
for forskningsomr├źdet, gennem en sammenligning af forskellige teknikker samt sp├”nd-
ingsberegninger for skal-element-modeller. En beregningsm├”ssigt effektiv Bernoulli-Euler-
bj├”lkeelement-model viser sig at v├”re en god tiln├”rmelse til den beregningsm├”ssigt dyrere
buede skalelementmodel, mens en ikke-buet skalelementmodel findes mindre pr├”cis. Dette
skyldes, at sidstn├”vnte overvurderer den radiale foliedeformations afh├”ngighed af den
aksiale koordinat, hvilket skyldes, at den ikke tager h├Ėjde for membran-sp├”ndingerne i
topfolien.

1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
Bearing technology has played an important role in the evolution of human societies for
thousands of years. Scientific advancements have led to the development of bearings from
early plain bearings to the highly efficient bearings employed today.
One of the most
widely used types are fluid bearings [28] that are a category of bearings for which a fluid
film separates the journal from the bearing surface.
Of these, most are oil-lubricated
although the share of gas-lubricated bearings have been increasing within the last century.
Early gas-lubricated bearings were rigid and had high sensitivity to misalignment and high
requirements for machining tolerances [19], which limited the practical use of gas-lubricated
bearings until the invention of Gas Foil Bearings (GFBs). GFBs are a sub-type of gas-
lubricated bearings categorised by having a compliant foil structure attached to the inside
of the bearing housing. GFBs were developed and put into use in turbo-machinery during
the 1950s and 1960s [6]. During the second half of the 1900s, GFBs gained popularity
within a number of fields [28], and were primarily known for their use within aircraft
air cycle machines [3]. Research was driven by the significant performance improvement
seen in military aircraft applications [2, 3, 6]. From the late 1960s until the late 1980s,
GFBs gradually replaced oil-lubricated and rolling element bearings in cooling, heating,
and pressurisation systems in most civil and military aircraft [2, 6] as well as various
ground vehicles, including military tanks [2, 6]. The first bump-type foil configuration was
patented in the mid-1970s and have since become the most widely used type of compliant
foil structure [2, 6]. Significant research advancements within the past couple of decades
include increased load capacity, extension of operating temperature range, and advances
within solid lubricant coatings; the sum of which have led to performance improvements
as well as increased the range of applications of GFBs [6, 15, 17, 20, 21, 27, 44]. Today,
important applications include air-cycle machines [2, 6, 6, 24, 27], machinery in clean
environments [6, 17, 25], computer disk drives [14], as well as high-speed machinery [2, 6,
17, 24, 24, 25].
Current research seeks to overcome some of the challenges of current GFB technology.
GFBs requires a high velocity in order to generate a consistent fluid film to separate
bearing and journal. As a result, wear occurs at start and stop [7, 17, 25], and the bearing
type is best suited for high velocities [17, 25, 27]. The dynamical stability of the bearing
type is compromised by low damping due to low lubricant viscosity [17], an inherently
cross-coupled stiffness [17, 25], and sub-synchronous vibrations [27]. The modelling of
GFBs is challenged by the compressibility of the lubricant, the compliant foil, and the
multi-domain nature of the system [21].
Furthermore, GFBs have relatively low load
carrying capacity when compared with other bearing types and require high precision in
both production and assembly [17, 25, 27].
Interest in GFBs continues to grow as they offer a combination of qualities that make them
uniquely qualified for a range of applications. The most commonly employed lubricant for
GFBs is atmospheric air. Its abundance in most operating environments means that no
lubrication system is needed for aerodynamic operation [6, 17, 24]. Another advantage of
using air as a lubricant is that the risk of working fluid contamination for e.g. air cycle
machines and clean environment machines is eliminated [17, 25]. GFBs are mechanically
simple when compared to oil-lubricated bearings as well as rolling-element bearings. They
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
9
==PAGE:20==
require less maintenance, weigh less, and are more reliable [2, 6]. For oil-lubricated bear-
ings, this is primarily caused by them necessitating a lubrication system whether under
hydrostatic or hydrodynamic operation. Where fluid bearings have negligible wear at con-
tinual use due to the lack of contact between bearing and shaft [7], ball bearings are subject
to more extensive wear and subsequently require more maintenance. Furthermore, ball
bearings consist of a higher amount of high-precision parts, increasing complexity. GFBs
are a low-noise alternative to other bearing types [24], are insensitive to work fluid con-
tamination by dust, sand, etc. [2], and are temperature-insensitive [2, 6, 24] compared to
oil-lubricated bearings and rolling-element bearings. Active Magnetic Bearings (AMBs)
are the primary competitors for GFBs in many applications due to the lack of oil, low
friction, and little need for maintenance. The primary advantage of GFBs over AMBs is
that the latter requires back-up bearings, where, in the event of a bearing failure, the low
clearances and tolerances of GFBs inherently limit the resulting damage for [2], as well as
control systems, which increase complexity, cost, and space requirements [8].
In recent years, the research into Hybrid Gas Foil Bearings (HGFBs) has increased, and
improvement opportunities have been found within load carrying capacity, stability limits,
damping characteristics, and low-velocity operation [28, 47]. HGFBs are GFBs modified
to include hybridisation using radial injection of pressurised gas. While this would increase
the complexity of the bearing type, primarily by requiring a lubrication system, overcoming
the current challenges of GFBs would significantly increase the range of applications for
GFBs.
Especially within turbomachinery, it is increasingly relevant and attractive to
venture into hybridisation [28].
1.2
Problem statement
The primary objective of the current work is the derivation and implementation of a
HGFB model including the actuator dynamics of piezoelectric injectors that balances
high accuracy with computational efficiency towards simulation of HGFBs with feedback
control.
As the current project seeks to prepare the implementation for inclusion of a controller,
it is of interest to include four injectors instead of three as used in [47] and appendix A.
By using four injectors, the vertical and horizontal directions will be decoupled, which is
advantageous when designing a controller. Thus, the inclusion of four injectors into the
code as well as testing the inclusion is likewise an important goal. Several aspects of the
number of pads per GFB have been researched in [16]. The primary contribution of the
current work towards this goal is the investigation and comparison of top foil modelling
techniques as well as the derivation and implementation of a stress components calculation
scheme for shell element top foil models.
10
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:21==
2
Theory
This chapter introduces theoretical concepts that are important for the understanding of
the present work, and that might be unfamiliar to the reader. Furthermore, this chapter
introduces case specific data used throughout the project. Please note that theory which
is only used in a specific chapter of the thesis can be found in the dedicated chapter.
2.1
Bearing types
The present work concerns itself with fluid bearings lubricated with gases and with a
bearing surface consisting of a compliant foil structure. This bearing type is termed a
Gas Foil Bearing (GFB). When capable of both aerostatic and aerodynamic operation, as
is the case for the present work, the bearing type is termed a Hybrid Gas Foil Bearing
(HGFB).
The working principle of a fluid bearing is that a fluid film must be pressurised in order
for the bearing and shaft to separate. In order to generate the necessary pressure in the
moving fluid film, either pressurised air must be supplied from an external source, or the
movement of the fluid film, caused by the relative movement of shaft and bearing surface,
and the variations in the film height in the circumferential direction must generate the
pressure profile. Based on the source of the fluid film pressure, the bearing is categorised
as either:
ŌĆó a fluid-static bearing, meaning that pressurised air is supplied to the bearing,
ŌĆó a fluid-dynamic bearing, meaning that the velocity of the rotor relative to the bearing
generates the necessary pressure to separate the two, or
ŌĆó a hybrid bearing, meaning that the bearing is capable of both static and dynamic
operation, i.e.
that the bearing can operate both with and without a supply of
pressurised air.
2.2
Piezoelectricity and piezoelectric stack actuators
The piezoelectric effect, also termed the direct piezoelectric effect, is the phenomenon that
certain materials respond to an applied mechanical stress by generating electric charge.
These materials are called piezoelectric materials. The inverse, or converse, piezoelectric
effect is a term used to describe that piezoelectric materials subject to an electric charge
will generate mechanical stress.
If unconstrained, this will result in an elongation or
contraction of the material. The direction depends on whether the applied electric field
and the poling direction have the same polarity [11].
Due to their small scale and their high speed, piezoelectric actuators are, in general, used
as a positioning mechanism for applications requiring high precision while not requiring
large movement [12]. One way to increase the actuator stroke is to stack a number of
electrically connected piezoelectric plates that will expand and contract in the thickness
direction of each piezoelectric layer, thereby increasing or decreasing the overall stack
length, i.e. increasing the actuator stroke.
2.3
Simple Elastic Foundation Model (SEFM)
A Simple Elastic Foundation Model, or a Winkler foundation, is a model used for describing
a compliant structure. The model consists of a number of springs or a number of spring-
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
11
==PAGE:22==
damper pairs, all independent of one another. The underlying assumptions are:
ŌĆó that top foil and bump foil are inseparable [21],
ŌĆó that the foil is subject to uniform axial deformation [21],
ŌĆó that stiffness variations due to friction effects are negligible [20], and
ŌĆó that stiffness and loss factor nonlinearities are negligible [20].
The Simple Elastic Foundation Model (SEFM) is generally applied when modelling the
bump foil in a GFB.
2.4
The G├╝mbel condition
While the pressures in the fluid film in GFBs will generally be superambient, subambient
pressures can occur. Opposite to how superambient pressures lead to an axially outward
deformation of the foil, subambient pressures can lead to a separation of the top foil and
the bump foil, which will equalise the pressure [20, 21].
As mentioned in section 2.3,
the SEFM assumes that the top foil and the bump foil are inseparable. Thereby, left
unchecked, subambient pressures can compromise the model.
A simple way of handling subambient pressures mathematically is to discard subambient
pressures during integration. This method is termed the G├╝mbel condition. For the code
presented in the current work, this is done during the dynamic pressure integration [46].

uses the data listed in table 2.2. The system and bearing parameters are defined in the
schematics in fig. 2.1. The injector geometry can be seen in fig. 2.2. A parameter map
using the parameters and levels shown in table 2.3 [47] is used, see also chapter 3. The
system simulated is a rigid rotor supported by two four-pad HGFBs.

3
Methods and materials
The numerical analyses are carried out using a multi-domain numerical model. The code
is developed by Sebastian von Osmanski during his PhD-thesis, [28]. The programme is
titled MRE_FVM_solve [42] using acronyms Modified Reynolds Equation (MRE) and Finite
Volume Method (FVM), and is described in [41, 47]. The code is based on programming
languages C and Python 3, where the Python-code primarily contains input-files, plotting
routines, and scripts to obtain specific combinations of output data. The Suitesparse
and Sundials libraries are used for handling sparse matrices and solving the system of
equations respectively.
The case-data used in this work comes from the experimental setup originally from Siemens
described in [19] and subsequently investigated in [18, 20, 21, 25, 36, 37, 44, 47], see
also section 2.5. The injector system case data used in this work is introduced in [26],
investigated in [30, 31, 31, 39], and detailed in [47], see also section 2.5.
Furthermore, a fluid mapping is used for obtaining mass flows due to injection. The fluid
mapping described in [47] is used. It is generated using the code developed by Sebastian
von Osmanski titled hybrid_injector_3d [43]. The code is based on programming lan-
guage Python 3 as well as the CFD programme OpenFOAM (versions v1906 and v1912
are tested).
The MRE_FVM_solve programme has been augmented and added to during this project, in
order to compute stress components, cf. chapter 6, include a piezoelectric stack actuator
model, cf. chapter 4, and prepare the code for inclusion of controllable injection flow, cf.
chapter 4.
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
17
==PAGE:28==
18
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:29==
4
Modelling
4.1
Motivation
As detailed in chapter 1, a number of the challenges faced by GFBs could be alleviated by
introducing hybrid operation, e.g. by reducing wear during start and stop, and increasing
the load carrying capacity. It is expected that active control could enhance the damping
capabilities of the bearing [28].
The primary contribution of this work is the incorporation of a piezoelectric stack actuator
model, as presented by Stefano Morosi in [26], into the GFB model developed and im-
plemented by Sebastian von Osmanski and presented in various works including [41, 47].
Hereby, the programme will be ready for implementation of a feedback control scheme.
This chapter outlines the general framework and details the injector model developed by
Sebastian von Osmanski, introduces the model of the piezoelectric stack actuator intro-
duced by Stefano Morosi, and presents and discusses a combined model. Furthermore,
nomenclature across authors and articles is compared.
4.2
Nomenclature
In the literature of the research group, there has been some discrepancies in symbol use
and naming conventions for important variables and constants relating to the injector. As
this work seeks to combine and consolidate the work carried out by former members of
the research group, a brief overview of the different conventions is given and a standard
for the present and future work is respectively presented and proposed.
4.2.1
Pressures
Most notably, the pressures have been subject to several conventions. The conventions
established in the three most important works for the present project are shown in fig. 4.1.
The convention used in [26], which presents the model for the piezoelectric stack actuator,
can be seen in fig. 4.1a. Here, the supply pressure is denoted psup, the pressure in the
nozzle above the pin is denoted pinj, and the pressure in the fluid film in the journal bearing
psup

is denoted pJB. In the follow-up article, [31], the supply pressure is denoted both psup and
pinj. This is illustrated in fig. 4.1b. In the article outlining the methodology for including
non-controlled injection, [47], the pressure at the inlet to the fluid volume analysed by a
CFD model is denoted pinj, whereas the pressure at the outlet of the fluid volume analysed
is denoted p’¼ülm, as seen in fig. 4.1c.
In the current work, it is proposed to preserve the original nomenclature for the pressure in
the nozzle above the pin, pinj, although this pressure is not used in the current work, and
for the supply pressure psup, c.f. fig. 4.2 for the suggested nomenclature for the present
and future works. It is argued that the pressure called pinj in [47] is a modified supply
pressure. This pressure is proposed denoted psup,mod. Please note that it could also be
seen as a modified injection pressure, where the injection pressure is defined as in [26] and
fig. 4.1a. Here, it is also relevant to address that this pressure is not equal to either the
supply pressure psup or the injection pressure pinj, as defined by fig. 4.2, and likely unknown
by the experimenter in an experimental setting. In order to remedy this, several courses
of action could be taken, e.g. expanding the CFD model to also contain (part of) the
supply pressure inlet, or assuming that a correction factor or mathematical expression can
relate psup and psup,mod and determining this factor or expression through experiments or
simulation. As the current work can be carried out regardless of the accuracy of psup,mod,
it is assumed that psup,mod is known. The nomenclature for the pressure at the outlet of
the fluid volume analysed using an external CFD model is carried over from [47].
4.3
Model prior to inclusion of actuator
This section outlines the model used prior to inclusion of the actuator. The inclusion of
the actuator in this framework is the main objective of the current thesis, and the subject
of the remaining sections of the present chapter.
The computer implementation of the theoretical mathematical model is a multi-domain
numerical model that assembles and solves the coupled state space residual equations.
While the computer implementation is capable of modelling ball bearings as well as rigid
bearings, the primary focus of the present work, as well as the implementation, is its
ability to model GFBs, i.e. its ability to model a rotor supported by bearings of which at
least one is a fluid film bearing with compliant foil separating the rotor from the bearing
housing, and using as lubricant a gas whose compressibility is affecting its performance.

Furthermore, the recent inclusion of hybridisation allowing for modelling of HGFBs is
essential to the present work.
A sketch of a four-pad HGFB introducing the primary parts of the system can be seen
in fig. 4.3a. Here, the rotor, the fluid film separating rotor and foil, the foil consisting of
the bump foil and top foil, as well as the bearing housing, are highlighted. Likewise, the
injectors can be seen, and the components of an injector can be seen in fig. 4.3b. The
bearing is operating based on a flow restriction control strategy, see section 4.3.4 for an
explanation of the injector principle of operation.
The existing model links three domains: the rotor domain, the foil domain, and the fluid
film domain. The underlying methodology used for modelling and simulating aerodynamic
GFBs is detailed in [41], while the inclusion of non-regulated and non-controlled injection,
thereby describing modelling and simulation of HGFBs, is described in [47]. While the
reader is referred to these works for an in-depth understanding of the methods and results
presented in these, a brief examination of essential assumptions, as well as an introduction
to the model, are considered fundamental to understanding the present work and its
function.

the gas film pressure, and ╦£rr is the rotor residual vector. See [47] for nondimensionalization
and equations for obtaining the bearing force vector.
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
21
==PAGE:32==
The bearing force vector can likewise be represented as [47]:
╦£fb = ╦£fb0 + H fb,p( ╦£pAB) ╦£pAB, ╦£pAB =

╦£pT
A
╦£pT

(4.3)
where ╦£fb0 is the constant part of the mapping, and H fb,p is the mapping matrix.
It is assumed that the rigid film height contribution from rotor movement is axially uniform
[47].
4.3.2
Foil domain
The compliant foil structure consists of top foil supported by bump foil. The bump foil
is modelled as an SEFM, with a loss factor ╬Ęb and a stiffness k.
Effects of potential
separation of bump and top foil are thus assumed negligible, and the G├╝mbel condition
is used for handling sub-ambient pressures. It is of interest to use EulerŌĆōBernoulli (EB)
beam elements to model the top foil in the present work due to two primary reasons. First,
it is the precedent set by the bulk of Sebastian von OsmanskiŌĆÖs work, including the work of
primary importance to the present work [41, 47]. Second, due to the inherent problems of
shell elements, section 5.1. Here, a foil model using EB beam elements is thus introduced.
In appendix A, this model was found to be a good approximation to a singly curved shell
element model for a three-pad HGFB. In chapter 5, the validity of using this approach for
a four-pad HGFB is investigated.
For a top foil model consisting of EB beam elements, the top foil consists of nfe foil
elements per bearing, where each node adds two DOF. The leading edge of each pad is
clamped. Thus, the foil DOF vector ╦£x f contains nfdof = 2nfe foil DOF describing the top
foil structure node displacements and rotations.
Thus, the state space residual form of the foil model equations of motion is given by [47]:
╦£D f ╦Ö╦£x f,╬▒ + ╦£K f ╦£x f,╬▒ ŌłÆ╦£fp,╬▒ = ╦£r f,╬▒ ŌłłR2nfe
(4.4)
where ╦£K f is the foil stiffness matrix, ╦£D f is the foil damping matrix, ╦£fp,╬▒ is the foil load
vector, and ╦£r f,╬▒ is the foil residual vector.
The stiffness matrix has contributions from both top and bump foil, while the damping
matrix is only contributed to by the SEFM alone [47]. See [47] for further elaboration,
including element matrices.
The foil load vector can be found as [47]:
╦£fp,╬▒ = ╦£fp0 + H fp,p ╦£p╬▒
(4.5)
where ╦£fp0 is the constant part of the mapping, H fp,p is the mapping matrix, and ╦£p╬▒ is the
Control Volume (CV) centre pressures in the fluid film.
4.3.3
Fluid domain
It is assumed that the fluid exhibits ideal gas behaviour, can be considered isoviscous, and
that the system processes are isothermal.
For describing the fluid film, the Modified Reynolds Equation is discretised using a Finite
Volume (FV) approach [41, 47] and a non-uniform Cartesian grid in order to refine the
mesh at, and close to, injection areas, see fig. 5.3 for the FV mesh for the first pad. The
fluid film has circumferential coordinate ╬Ė and axial coordinate ╦£z.
22
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:33==
Please note that the energy equation has not been included, thereby presenting a source
of error [41, 47]. A Central Differencing Scheme (CDS) is used for obtaining pressure gra-
dients from CV centre pressures, and pressure values are obtained using a Linear Upwind
Differencing Scheme (LUDS) [41, 47].
Each bearing is discretised using nCV = n╬Ė,FVnz cells, yielding the fluid film DOF vector
╦£p╬▒ for each bearing containing the CV centre pressures for bearing ╬▒.
Thus, the state space residual form of the fluid film FV equations is given by [41, 47]:
AFV( ╦£p╬▒, ╦£xr, ╦£x f,╬▒) ╦£p╬▒ + BFV(╦Ö╦£xr, ╦Ö╦£x f,╬▒) ╦£p╬▒ + CFV(╦£xr, ╦£x f,╬▒) ╦Ö╦£p╬▒ + ╦Ö╦£m╬▒ = ╦£rFV,╬▒
(4.6)
where AFV relates to the Couette and Poiseuille terms, BFV relates to the squeeze term,
CFV relates to the local expansion term, ╦Ö╦£m╬▒ holds the CV injection flow contributions,
and ╦£rFV,╬▒ is the FV residual vector.
4.3.4
Injection
As discussed, the injection framework is fundamental to the present work why it is detailed
in the present section.
Injector operating principle
A sketch introducing the components of the injector valve can be seen in fig. 4.3b. The
valve is controlling the flow of lubricant supply from an external pressurised gas supply
through a tube marked by ŌĆØsupply pressureŌĆØ in the figure, and through the cone section
into the bearing gas film. By increasing the voltage over the piezoelectric stack actuator,
the stack will expand, pushing the plastic pin inwards radially, reducing or preventing air
flow by reducing or closing the gap in the cone section. When the voltage level is decreased,
the Belleville washers will cause the stack to contract, pushing the plastic pin outwards
radially, increasing the air flow to the bearing. The O-ring helps seal the mechanism,
reducing pressure loss.
Assumptions
The injector model is subject to several assumptions as described by von Osmanski in [47],
namely that:
1. the injection flow is assumed laminar and steady,
2. the MRE fluid film pressure along the circumference of a circle originating from the
injector centre and with radius r’¼ülm is approximately uniform, and
3. film height variations within the injection zone are negligible.
Parameter map
Sebastian von Osmanski presented a framework for simulation of HGFBs with radial injec-
tion of pressurised air in his article [47]. The framework allows for flow restriction control
via regulation of the injector valve clearance hinj, cf. fig. 5.2, and/or the modified supply
pressure psup,mod, c.f. fig. 4.2. Please note that the latter is denoted injection pressure pinj
in [47], see discussion on nomenclature in section 4.2.1.
In the programme behind the analysis carried out in [47], the injector valve clearance hinj
and the modified supply pressure psup,mod for each injector remained static throughout a
simulation, as the model does not allow these values to vary over time. This also means
that the injection is necessarily non-controlled in this implementation.
As discussed in [47], injection flow in HGFBs are typically described using analytical
expressions augmented by correction factors based on e.g. CFD models. The approach
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
23
==PAGE:34==
presented in [47] differs by instead using results of a CFD simulation of a sub-model
of the injector and the fluid film close to the injector. The advantage of this approach
is its ability to take into account the interdependence between factors, compressibility,
and viscous shear [47]. While the framework should be able to also take into account
turbulence and unsteady flow, the injection flow is assumed laminar and steady in the
current implementation [47].
A parameter map is generated once for a given injector
geometry by carrying out a factorial experiment, obtaining injected mass flows for all
possible combinations of all levels of all factors using CFD results from a simulation within
the injector geometry.
The factors expected to influence the mass flow and therefore
investigated in [47] were: the modified supply pressure, the rotor angular velocity, the
fluid film pressure, the journal eccentricity, and the nozzle clearance. The latter is also
termed the injector valve clearance. It was found that the angular velocity of the rotor had
negligible influence on the effective mass flow rate [47]. The levels that were investigated
can be seen in table 2.3.
As described in chapter 3, the parameter map generated for [47] is used. The results are
based on the following assumptions:
1. that all walls have a constant temperature Tiso,
2. that the injection flow is laminar and steady,
3. and that the MRE fluid film pressure is uniform along the sub-model radius from
the centre of the injector [47].
Introducing injector mass flow in the model
The generated parameter map data is stored in a file accessed by the GFB simulation
programme, interpolating to obtain mass flow rate based on the parameters or factors
described in table 2.3. The interpolation scheme used is a piece-wise multi-linear scheme
described in [48], cf. [47]. As described in [47], the mass flow through the ninj injectors in
bearing ╬▒ can be written as:
╦Ö╦£minj,╬▒ =
 ╦Ö╦£minj,1
...

(4.7)
where finj indicates interpolation, hinj,╬▒ is the injector valve clearance for each injector in
bearing ╬▒, psub,mod,╬▒ is the modified supply pressure for each injector in bearing ╬▒, p’¼ülm,╬▒ is
the Boundary Condition (BC) fluid film pressure in the CFD sub-model for each injector
in bearing ╬▒, ╬Ąx,╬▒ is the equivalent injector eccentricity in bearing ╬▒, and Ōä”is the angular
velocity of the rotor.
The first four independent variables are vectors of length ninj, whereas the rotor angular
velocity is a scalar.
The interpolation scheme likewise provides the derivatives of the
function with respect to each of the function arguments [47].
Once this interpolation has been carried out, the CV injection flow in bearing ╬▒ can be

4.4
Actuator model and domain
The values for actuator constants can be found in table 2.2.
4.4.1
Piezoelectric force
The piezoelectric stack actuator force model used in the present work is presented in [26].
Herein, the resultant piezoelectric force Fpiezo is given by:
Fpiezo (u(t), y(t)) = kpiezocpiezoNstacku(t) ŌłÆkpiezoy(t)
(4.15)
where Fpiezo is the resultant piezoelectric force, kpiezo is the elastic stiffness of the piezo-
electric stack, cpiezo is the piezoelectric coupling coefficient, u(t) is the feeding voltage,
Nstack is the number of piezoelectric material layers in the piezoelectric stack, and y(t) is
the linear displacement of the pin. Thus, the piezoelectric force consists of a contribution
from the voltage, causing the piezoelectric stack to expand more by increasing the volt-
age, and a contribution that reduces the piezoelectric force with increased distance to the
original position.
4.4.2
Piezoelectric stack actuator model
In [26], a simplified model of the piezoelectric stack actuator is introduced. The model
presented in the present work is equivalent with two exceptions: first, the coordinate
system originates from the pin mass instead of from the hinj = 0 position, and second, the
term kpiezoy(t) is negative when included in the force equilibrium equation. The latter is
expected to be a typographical error.
Using the coordinate system seen in fig. 4.4 for a piezoelectric stack actuator, a mathe-
matical model is developed. Based on the coordinate system of fig. 4.4, the injector valve
clearance is given by:
hinj = hinj,max ŌłÆy(t)
(4.16)
where hinj,max is the maximum injector valve clearance. From fig. 4.4, it can likewise be
seen that the dynamics of a single piezoelectric stack actuator can be described by:
mpin ┬©y(t) = Fpiezo ŌłÆkmechy(t) ŌłÆdmech ╦Öy(t)
(4.17)
where mpin is the mass of the pin, dmech is the equivalent viscous friction coefficient of
Belleville washers and O-rings, and kmech is the elastic stiffness of the Belleville washers.

4.4.3
Literature review
The model for the piezoelectric force presented by Morosi in [26] is similar to the one
presented for piezoelectric actuators in [40]. Although a coordinate system is not defined
in [40], it appears that the y(t)-value (denoted s(t) in [40]) is defined from the compressed,
u(t) = 0 V position, and is positive in the direction of expansion. The part of the expression
directly related to the input voltage is furthermore in accordance with what is presented
in [38].
These linear models of piezoelectricity can be derived from the linear constitutive law for
piezoelectric materials based on linear theory of piezoelectricity as standardised by the
ANSI-IEEE 176 Standard of Piezoelectricity [4]. These constitutive relations are widely
used and recognised [1, 11, 13, 29].
As addressed by Morosi in [26], this model does not account for hysteresis nonlinearities
[1, 11, 13, 26] nor nonlinearities of other kinds [13], including creep [11]. In [26], Mo-
rosi suggests referring to [11] for compensation methods if hysteresis nonlinearities are
of importance for the system analysed. It is noted by several sources [1], that hystere-
sis nonlinearities affect all piezoelectric actuators, and experimental results suggest that
hysteresis significantly deteriorates results when using piezoelectric stack actuators in a
feedback loop with voltage amplifiers [23].
Another critique of the standard of piezoelectricity presented in [4] is that it does not
describe the actuator dynamics [1]. However, the solution suggested by [1] is to describe
the mechanical behaviour of the actuator using a single DOF mass-spring-damper system,
as is done by Morosi, who combines the presented constitutive laws, in the form of the
piezoelectric force, with a one DOF model of the actuator in order to take this into account
[26]. A similar model is used to describe the dynamics of the mechanical system in [22].
[13] postulates that the limitations of the model presented in [4] is not accurate enough
for use in position or force control requiring accuracy. Several alternative strategies have
been suggested.
[13] suggests using a lumped-parameter, energy-based approach, and
[1] suggests combining a mechanical model similar to the one presented in [26] with a
first-order differential equation aimed at modelling the hysteresis effects.
In conclusion, the model suggested by Morosi is in accordance with what is generally imple-
mented by a significant amount of the literature. The assumed linearity of the piezoelectric
stack actuator, however, is critiqued for having limitations in real-world applications due
to the inherent nonlinearities of piezoelectric materials, particularly hysteresis nonlinear-
ities. It should be noted, however, that high-quality modern actuators often encompass
position sensors and internal feedback loops, significantly reducing the effect of hysteresis
nonlinearities. For an example of these actuators, see [5]. While the degree of effect of
hysteresis on the present application is unknown, it is deemed outside the scope of the
current project. It is suggested that an investigation of the nonlinear effects in the system
and their degree of influence could be carried out, and, if nonlinearities are found to be of
significance, that it will be possible to augment the implementation sought in the present
work by adding these to the model.
4.5
Coupled system state space formulation
In the present work, a case with two four-pad bearings is used. This section outlines the
inclusion of the associated states in the coupled system state space formulation.

new injector states.

Terms related to the input vector has been added separately in the vector u, fG,u(u), as
these terms can belong to either of the other three parts depending on the definition of the
input signal u╬▒. In the current model, the input is an external force, i.e. it is independent
of the state vector, z, and the state vector differentiated with respect to time, ╦Öz. For a
given time-step, the input is constant.
Once a feedback control scheme has been implemented, it will be dependent on the system
states.

4.7
Implementation
4.7.1
Non-dimensionalization
The system is non-dimensionalized prior to inclusion in the coupled system state space
residual equation.
The system prior to inclusion of injection uses the following scales. The foil domain and
the fluid domain use radial length scaling factor equal to the radial clearance C, in-film or
circumferential length scaling factor equal to the radius of the bearing R, pressure scaling
equal to the ambient pressure pa, and time scaling factor equal to one divided by the
rotor angular velocity Ōä”if a positive rotational speed is given. The latter is used as the
system-wide time scaling factor for non-zero rotor angular velocity. For a non-positive
rotational speed, unity is used as time scale. The rotor system uses mass scaling factor
equal to the rotor mass mr, length scaling factor 1 mm, and derived force scale equal to
the length scaling factor times the mass scaling factor divided by the global system time
scaling factor squared.
32
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:43==
Thus, it is suggested that the global system time scaling factor is used for the actuator
domain as well. Furthermore, as the scaling factors are primarily system characteristics
of the same or a similar order of magnitude as the variables and constants of interest, the
maximum injector valve clearance hinj,max is proposed as a reasonable length scaling factor
for the actuator domain.
In order to non-dimensionalize the system matrix Ainj, the standard SI units of its com-
ponents are analysed:

ŌłÆkpiezo + kmech
mpin

= 1
s2
(4.41)
and:

ŌłÆdmech
mpin

= 1
s
(4.42)
In order to obtain a non-dimensional system matrix, these elements are thus scaled by the
time scaling factor squared and the time scaling factor respectively.
In order to non-dimensionalize the input matrix Binj, the standard SI units of its compo-
nents are analysed:
[ku] = sA
kgm
(4.43)
and when multiplied by the input voltage:
[kuu(t)] = m
s2
(4.44)
As it is of numerical interest to include the scaling offline while assembling the con-
stant matrices instead of continually augmenting the input voltage directly, the non-
dimensionalization of the term Binju╬▒ is carried out in the code by scaling the voltage
constant ku by a derived acceleration scaling factor equal to the length scale divided by
the time scaling factor squared, thereby obtaining a scaled voltage constant, and input
matrix Binj, with units
s3A
kgm2 = VŌłÆ1.
The mapping matrix Hhinj,xinj and the vector of ones used in the mapping from injector DOF
vector to injector clearance vector are inherently non-dimensional, while the maximum
injector valve clearance is non-dimensionalized by dividing it with the length scaling factor,
i.e. obtaining ╦£hinj,max = 1.
4.7.2
Initial conditions
The initial conditions for the actuator displacement DOF are chosen so as to simulate
fully open injectors corresponding to y(t) = u(t) = 0 and hinj = hinj,max for transient
simulations. Alternative approaches could be to take as input a user-defined initial position
of the actuators or to use as initial conditions the first values in the functions controlling
the actuator movement. However, as the end goal of the implementation is a feedback loop
control scheme, these methods would have little practical usage in the final programme.
The initial conditions for the actuator velocity DOF are set to zero for all injectors.
For steady state simulations, the actuator velocity DOF are likewise set to zero for all
injectors, whereas the actuator displacement DOF initial values are calculated from the
user-specified hinj-values.
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques

==PAGE:44==
4.7.3
Maximum input voltage
Theoretical value
When solving the steady state system, fG,t of eq. (4.33) is set to zero.
Thus, for the
actuator system equations, we obtain:

As the residual is sought minimised numerically and should equate to zero analytically,

For the maximum displacement of the pin of xi1 = 45 ┬Ąm corresponding to a fully closed
injector, this yields a maximum input voltage of 21
26 V Ōēł0.81 V. In two of the articles
treating the injector system, namely [30, 32], the maximum input voltage is defined as
10 V. The remaining articles do not specify a maximum input voltage. The cause of this
is that the values given for the piezoelectric actuators in table 2.2 taken from [26] are for
an example case and not values corresponding to a physical injector. However, as these
parameters are necessary for the model, and as no alternative values are accessible, the
maximum input voltage that solves eq. (4.48) is used in the present work.
Numerical implementation
The programme was not able to converge for input voltage values u(t) too close to the
maximum voltage value umax. For voltage values less than or equal to 0.9975umax, no
issues were experienced, while using a value of 0.998umax meant that the programme did
not converge. It is assumed that this is caused either by tolerances, numerical errors,
or similar inaccuracies in the programme or significant extrapolation from the parameter
map due to the very small clearance resulting in problematic values of mass fluxes or a
different variable. The problem has not been identified. However, the problem has been
circumvented by setting all voltages larger than 0.9975umax equal to this value, and setting
mass fluxes and differentiated mass fluxes equal to zero for injector valve clearances less
than 0.5 % of the maximum injector valve clearance hinj,max.
4.8
Case
The system and values presented in section 2.5 are used as a test case. The data tables
are supplied by table 4.1 Furthermore, as discussed in chapter 5, an EB beam top foil
model is used. A pressure psup,mod of 300 kPa or 3 bar is used throughout unless otherwise
stated.

4.9.2
Transient results
Throughout this section, only the results for bearing A are shown as the results from the
two bearings are equivalent. Figures with equivalent data for bearing B can be found in
appendix C.
The results of comparing the eccentricities after 0.9 s, i.e. 0.3 s after the step function from
fully closed injector(s) to half-open injector(s) and before a sine function is introduced,
can be seen in fig. 4.6b. The numbering of the injectors in each bearing can be seen in
fig. 4.6a, and data for the simulation runs can be found in figs. 4.7 and 4.8. Here, the
input voltage, injector valve clearance, actuator states, and rotor states are plotted against
time.
Comparing injection through each of the injectors individually to the run without injection,
it can be seen that the resulting eccentricities in the y-directions are pushed in the direction
of the inflow of air. In the x-direction, the same tendency is seen for injectors 2 and 4, i.e.
the injectors that are closest to lying on the vertical x-axis. Injectors 1 and 3 are lying
on a line closer to the horizontal y-axis. Injector 1, that lies above the y-axis, results in
lifting the rotor slightly, while injector 3, that lies below the y-axis, results in pushing the
rotor slightly downwards.
While no comprehensive and rigorous timing of transient experiments have been carried
out, it was seen for the individual simulation runs for injectors 1, 2, and 4 that inclusion

==PAGE:48==
of the actuator domain increased the necessary computational time with approximately

the simulation run for all injectors simultaneous increased the computational time with
50 % and 75 % respectively.
Figs. 4.9-4.13 investigates the system response to the transient input function including
the sine function with various amplitudes. In each figure, the smallest sine amplitude can
be seen on the leftmost sub-figure, while the largest sine amplitude can be seen on the
rightmost sub-figure.
It can be seen how the injector valve clearance is directly calculated
from the actuator displacement. Furthermore, it can be seen how these are a product of
the input voltage. The actuator velocity has a large impulse for the step at 0.6 s, the form
typical for a time derivative of a ramp function due to the ramp function, zero for constant
actuator displacement, and a cosine due to the sine function, i.e. the expected derivatives
for each actuator displacement section. It can be seen that the eccentricities varies with
injector case and sine amplitude. Throughout, it can be seen how a larger sine amplitude
give rise to more significant amplitudes of the 2 Hz vibrations that are caused by the sine
waves. It can also be seen that when all injectors are tested simultaneously, see fig. 4.9,
the largest effect is seen in the y-direction. Note that the y-direction is the horizontal
direction. Significant transient vibrations are seen for the step function. The same is true
of injector 3, see fig. 4.12, while the remaining three injectors have significantly smaller
amplitudes in this transient vibration. Particularly injector 4, see fig. 4.13, has very little
transient response. This is related to the smaller step experienced by the eccentricities
here. For injectors 1 and 3, cf. figs. 4.10 and 4.12 respectively, the largest effect on the
eccentricities is seen in the y-direction, while injectors 2 and 4, cf. figs. 4.11 and 4.13,
have the highest influence on the x-direction eccentricities compared to the y-direction
eccentricities. The orbit size of the steady state response appears to be equivalent for all
input values. Furthermore, no instabilities are seen.
Orbit plots corresponding to the five injection runs used for analysing eccentricities in figs.
4.6-4.8 can be found in figure 4.14a-e, while the non-injection run has orbit plot as seen
in fig. 4.15. Orbit plots corresponding to the fifteen injection runs that include a sine
function in the input, see figs. 4.9-4.13 can be found in figure 4.14f-t. In the upper left
corner, the initial response to system start can be seen with larger orbits for the initial
transient response. The ramp function is identified as a south-east directed chain or series
of consecutive orbits. At the end of this line, a higher concentration of orbits signifies the
system coming to rest at a position where all injectors are fully closed. A line connects
this position with the position of the rotor after the step function. The transient response
to this step function five rise to the lines that appear non-systematic in this area, while
the sine function give rise to the high concentration of orbits on a line. As expected, the
system with all injectors open results in movement along the line from the fully open to the
fully closed injectors. It can be seen that a higher sine amplitude results in a longer line
of sine-orbits, while the injection case determines the orientation of the line of sine-orbits
as well as their position in the bearing.
In fig. 4.16, one pair of eccentricity plots, specifically from fig. 4.12b, is shown in a larger
scale in order to allow the reader to better see the dominating vibration frequency for the
transient responses. In fig. 4.17, the result of a Fast Fourier Transform (FFT) analysis can
be seen. The rightmost peak corresponds to the rotor angular velocity. Comparing the
three plots, it can be seen that increasing the injection pressure results in a slight shift of
the peak at approximately 80 Hz. For a modified supply pressure of 2 bar, the peak lies
around 77.5 Hz for both bearings, for 2.5 bar, the peak lies around 80 Hz for both bearings,
and for 3 bar, the peak lies around 82.5 Hz for both bearings.

4.10
Discussion
The model used for the piezoelectric actuators does not take nonlinearities into account.
While a comprehensive model of any piezoelectric material should take hysteresis nonlin-
earities into account, modern professional-grade actuators are designed such that its effect
is minimised. Therefore, without investigating the degree to which nonlinearities affect
the system, it is argued that it might be detrimental rather than an improvement to the
model to include hysteresis nonlinearities.
It was found that the maximum voltage corresponding to the maximum displacement of
the actuator pin was significantly less than what was expected from previous works on
the actuator system.
This was found to be caused by the system parameters for the
piezoelectric actuators being a simulation case and not physical values for the system.
For transient simulations of a step function input, it was found that the actuators lying
close to the horizontal axis resulted in vertical eccentricity variations in the opposite
direction of their injection orientation, i.e. injector 1, that lies above the y-axis, results in
lifting the rotor slightly, while injector 3, that lies below the y-axis, results in pushing the
rotor slightly downwards. While this might appear counter-intuitive, the direction of the
rotor angular velocity as well as the proximity of the injectors to the y-axis are expected
to result in the contribution from injector 3 primarily affecting the area, by increasing
the pressure, above the rotor, while the contribution from injector 1 primarily affects the
area, by increasing the pressure, below the rotor. As a product of this, the results are
deemed plausible. It should be noted that varying the system values, e.g. by increasing
the modified supply pressure, might alter this behaviour. For the case with all injectors
open, the resulting change in eccentricities compared to the case without injection appears
to be a reasonable superposition of the individual injector cases. This is likewise deemed
plausible.
For transient simulations of a step function, a ramp function, and a sine function as well as
a constant input function, all system responses were in accordance with expected results.

49
==PAGE:60==
Comparing FFT responses for different injection pressure values, it was found that an
increase in pressure resulted in an increase of a natural frequency. This is in accordance
with the expected increase of fluid film stiffness for an increase of injection pressure.
4.11
Conclusion
A model of the piezoelectric actuator dynamics was introduced into the existing three-
domain theoretical and numerical model.
The steady state implementation was found
to be in accordance with the original implementation. It is likewise noteworthy that for
a steady state implementation, the inclusion of the actuator domain in the code did not
reduce the efficiency of the code measurably. As the efficiency of the code is of high import,
this is desirable. While similar results would be advantageous for transient operation, the
implementation does increase computational complexity here.
Depending on the run,
necessary time increased by 10 % to 75 %. The model was tested in transient simulation in
terms of the eccentricities, various constant values of the input voltage, a ramp function, a
step function, and a sine function. Sine amplitudes of 11 %, 22 %, and 27 % of the maximum
input voltage or injector valve clearance were tested for each of the four injector pairs as
well as all injectors at once. All results were in accordance with expected behaviour, which
evidence that the actuator domain has been successfully implemented in the programme.
4.12
A note on implementation
This section is primarily for the use of people working on the programme in the future.
The significant number of changes necessary in order to implement the actuator domain
means that it proved unfeasible to introduce all changes here. An overview of the changes
can be obtained by comparing code version signe_v6_actuator with signe_v5, the latter
of which includes the stress implementation but not the actuator implementation.
The affected files are:
1. actuatormesh.c/.h (new file)
2. file_IO.c/.h
3. fv.c/.h
4. indata_SiemensRig_inj.py
5. init_and_run.py
6. injection.c/.h
7. main.c/.h
8. plots.py
9. python_datastruct.c/.h
10. reader.py
11. statespace.c/.h
12. steady.c/.h
13. transient.c/.h
4.12.1
Troubleshooting
During this implementation, a problem occurred that I had not encountered before. I will
share the solution here in case someone else encounters it. The C-code did not rebuild
correctly giving error messages that a clock skew had been detected, that the C files had
modifications in the future, and that the build might not be complete. I solved the problem
by opening the terminal from the folder cmake-build-debug, entering the following two
lines in the terminal:
1
$ find . -exec touch {} \;
2
$ make clean
50
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:61==
5
Top foil modelling
5.1
Motivation
In appendix A, a paper investigating the influence of various top foil modelling techniques
can be found. The paper was motivated by the lack of agreement and consistency in top
foil modelling in the literature.
In the paper, four different modelling techniques were compared in terms of steady state
analysis efficiency and accuracy:
1. neglecting the top foil, modelling the entire foil structure using an SEFM,
2. modelling the top foil using EB beam elements supported by an SEFM,
3. modelling the top foil using shell elements supported by an SEFM, where the shell
structure has initial curvatures equal to infinity in the directions of both principal
curvatures, cf. fig. 5.1a, and
4. modelling the top foil using shell elements supported by an SEFM, where the shell
structure has one initial principal curvature equal to infinity and one equal to the
radius of the gas foil bearing, cf. fig. 5.1b.
In order to assist the reader visualising the difference between the two shell element models,
please see fig. 5.1 where the initial and steady state deformation of the foil structure has
been plotted for an example case.
Without injection, all four modelling techniques exhibited little discrepancies for journal
eccentricities, whereas it was found that the performance of the SEFM model was com-
promised when introducing injection. Using the latter of the four models as a benchmark,
it was found that the EB beam element model had superior performance compared to the
remaining two and comparable performance to the benchmark with and without injection.

Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
51
==PAGE:62==
Thus, this supports the use of EB beam elements to model the top foil instead of a full
shell element model. This significantly impacts the required computational expense as
well as avoids the significant disadvantages of shell elements.
As there are significant
discrepancies between the computational complexity of the EB beam model and the shell
element models, cf. appendix A, the insignificant differences in accuracy, both with and
without injection, has important indications for future models. Of even higher impor-
tance is the difficulty in implementing shell elements. Shell elements are prone to cause
ill-conditioning and have interaction between membrane and bending actions [10]. Fur-
thermore, the implementation of shell elements used in this project have problems with
element disorders, most noticeably locking [10]. Thus, most often, when shell elements are
needed, high-quality commercial software tailored to handle the various problems of the
element type is recommended (Sigmund, Ole, 01-03-2021, personal communication).
The paper in appendix A investigated a system with two three-pad GFBs. In order to
ease controller design and control using piezoelectric stack actuators, it is of interest to
decouple two perpendicular directions of motion by using four actuators per bearing, and
thereby four-pad GFBs. This chapter seeks to examine the validity of the findings outlined
in appendix A for a system with two four-pad GFBs.
Several other aspects of the performance of four-pad GFBs relative to three-pad GFBs have
been tested using the programme. The findings have been documented in [16]. Generally,
four-pad GFBs were found to be superior to three-pad GFBs.
5.2
Case
The rotor is modelled as rigid and supported by two four-pad GFBs. Case data is listed
in table 2.1.
The circumferential area without pads is kept constant in the transfer from three to four
pads. For three pads, each pad spans 115┬░ leaving 15┬░ free. Thus, for a four-pad imple-
mentation, each pad spans 86.25┬░.
The four cases without injection are simulated with air as lubricant under aerodynamic
operation. For the four-pad injection cases, the fourth injector, starting at the bearing
top point and counting in the positive direction, cf. fig. 4.6a, is open with injector valve
clearance hinj = 35 ┬Ąm, cf. fig. 5.2, and modified supply pressure psup,mod = 400 kPa.
The remaining three injectors are closed. Thus, the case investigated in this chapter has a
constant injector valve clearance, corresponding to the implementation prior to inclusion of
variable injector valve clearance and actuator model, see section 4.3. In order to determine
mass flow due to injection, a parameter-map approach is used as described in [47]. The
parameter map and the injector geometry used are calculated and presented in the article
[47] using the parameters and levels seen in table 2.3.
See section 5.3 for discretisation of fluid film and top foil mesh.
5.3
Discretisation and convergence
The convergence of the fluid film and foil meshes should be ensured prior to carrying out
simulations and obtaining results.
It was found by Sebastian von Osmanski, as documented in [47], that the Onset Speed of
Instability (OSI) converged slower than other system properties such as the equilibrium
position. Thus, for converged OSI, the equilibrium eccentricities will likewise be converged.
For the fluid film mesh, he found that the system with three-pad GFBs converged with
5 CVs across the injection zone, a grading of 5, 45 circumferential CVs per each of the
52
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:63==
hinj
Figure 5.2:
In-
jector model.
Figure 5.3: Non-uniform structured Cartesian FV mesh for the first
pad.
three pads, and 18 CVs in the axial direction, which, due to the symmetry condition, span
only half the bearing length L, and, due to the symmetry condition and the injection hole,
correspond to Nz = 15 in the code. The fluid film mesh for one pad can be seen in fig. 5.3.
Please note that the grid is symmetric around the lengthwise midpoint of the bearing.
The number of elements for the SEFM foil model corresponds to the number of CVs for
the fluid film mesh.
Furthermore, for the top foil, it was found that 5 beam elements per pad resulted in
converged OSIs for EB beam elements and three pads. This was doubled to 10 elements
per pad due to the low computational cost of the additional foil elements.
In the present project, the number of CVs and elements per pad in the circumferential
direction is kept constant from three to four pads, which should ensure convergence based
on the findings of Sebastian von Osmanski in [47].
The convergence of top foil elements for the shell element models is checked. In appendix A,
nl = 6 axial elements spanning half the bearing length and n╬Ė = 10 circumferential
elements per pad are used. In order to match the EB beam element model, these values
are kept constant per pad from three to four pads. The aspect ratio of the elements are
checked in order to make sure that element distortion increasing susceptibility to errors is
avoided [10]. The ratio of axial to circumferential element side lengths is increased from
approximately 0.6 to approximately 0.83.
Thus, as the aspect ratio is reasonable and
improved by the change, it is not a likely source of errors. In order to ensure continuous
convergence, it is required that existing nodes are not moved between iterations [10]. To
maintain a constant ratio between axial to circumferential element side lengths, these are
likewise varied simultaneously and regular refinement is used, cf. [10]. Thus, three cases
are run, nl = 3 and n╬Ė = 5, nl = 6 and n╬Ė = 10, and nl = 12 and n╬Ė = 20, checking for
convergence of the investigated parameter, eccentricity. The discrepancies between each
run in either direction is below 1 % for each increment, and below 0.3 % for the second
increment. Thus, the system is considered converged for nl = 6 and n╬Ė = 10.
5.4
Results
The sixteen cases are simulated, obtaining steady state journal eccentricities. The journal
eccentricities can be seen in table 5.1 and fig. 5.4. The case where the top foil is modelled
using curved shell elements is used as a benchmark as it is the least simplified, and thus
expected to be the most accurate, of the configurations investigated.

Of special interest is the performance of the EB beam element top foil model used through-

framework of the code.
It can be seen that with and without injection, the EB beam element model offers the best
approximation to the curved shell element model compared to the remaining two models.
Without injection, the deviations for each bearing are ŌłÆ0.19 % and 0.02 % in the x- and
y-directions respectively. With injection, the deviations for each bearing are ŌłÆ0.16 % and
0.09/0.1 % in the x- and y-directions respectively.

ŌłÆ0.55 % and ŌłÆ0.92 % in the x- and y-directions respectively without injection, and of
ŌłÆ0.50 % and ŌłÆ1.14 % in the x- and y-directions respectively with injection.
The SEFM model exhibits large deviations. Without injection, the deviations for bearing
A are ŌłÆ8.71 % and ŌłÆ35.9 % in the x- and y-directions respectively and the deviations
for bearing B are ŌłÆ8.29 % and ŌłÆ34.1 % in the x- and y-directions respectively.
With

respectively and the deviations for bearing B are ŌłÆ10.2 % and ŌłÆ34.1 % in the x- and
y-directions respectively.

It can be seen that the SEFM model fails to accurately or physically describe the behaviour
of the top foil, particularly for the second foil. Further investigation reveals that this is
caused by similar irregularities in the pressure found by the programme. However, these
irregularities in the pressure are not present for the remaining three models. The cause of
the problem has not been identified.
The remaining three models yield almost equivalent approximations of the axial average
of the radial foil deformation.
The foil deformation for the basic SEFM and the EB beam model can be seen in fig. 5.6a
and fig. 5.6b respectively.
Comparing the radial foil deformation of the basic SEFM and the EB beam model, it can
be seen that the SEFM model has zero displacement at the trailing edges of the pads.
This is caused by the assumption of the basic SEFM that the deflection of the springs of
the SEFM are independent of one another. Furthermore, it can be seen that the SEFM
model underestimates the foil deformation for a significant part of the circumference, and
has some discontinuities at the trailing edge of the second pad.
The radial foil deformation of the models with shell elements (cases 3 and 4) can likewise
be seen in fig. 5.6.
For the model with curved shell elements, cf.
figs. 5.6c and 5.6e,
it can be seen that the largest axial variations in radial deformation are approximately
from 5.7 ┬Ąm to 8.2 ┬Ąm, or equivalently from 2.2 ┬Ąm to 3.1 ┬Ąm. For the model with non-
curved shell elements, cf. figs. 5.6d and 5.6f, the largest axial variations are approximately
from 2.5 ┬Ąm to 8.9 ┬Ąm, or equivalently from 0.8 ┬Ąm to 3.4 ┬Ąm.
Similarly, the sub-zero
radial deformations vary significantly more for the model with non-curved shell elements
compared to the model with curved shell elements.
As observed in the article in appendix A, the discrepancies of the journal eccentricities
between the two shell models are assumed to be caused by the non-curved shell element
model exaggerating the axial dependency of the radial foil deformation. This is expected
to be caused by a failure to account for increased stiffness due to initial curvature. Physical
thin sheets exhibit dependency between longitudinal and transverse curvature [33]. Shell
elements account for this, unlike e.g. plate elements. Thus, by neglecting initial curvature,
the non-curved shell element model does not account for the phenomenon that curvature
along one principal radius of curvature increases the resistance to curvature along the sec-
ond principal radius of curvature. This can also be expressed as that the initial curvature
in the direction of one principal radius of curvature has a stiffening effect in the direction
of the second principal radius of curvature.
The discrepancies between the EB beam element model (case 2) and the curved shell
element model (case 4) are assumed caused by the assumption of uniform axial deflection
for the EB beam element model.
5.4.2
Foil deformations for case with injection
Here, the foil deformations of bearing A for the steady state analysis with injection are
presented. Figures with equivalent data for bearing B can be found in appendix D. There
are only insignificant differences between bearings.
In fig. 5.7, the one-dimensional models are plotted against the axial average of the shell
element models. Please note that the top part of the peak at the trailing edge of the second
foil has been excluded in order to ease comparison of the remaining models, see fig. 5.8a for
the complete foil deformation of the SEFM model. It can be seen that the SEFM model
fails to accurately or physically describe the behaviour of the top foil, particularly for the

second foil. Further investigation reveals that this is caused by similar irregularities in

The remaining three models yield almost equivalent approximations of the axial average

and fig. 5.8b respectively.

be seen that the SEFM model has zero displacement at the trailing edges of the pads.
This is caused by the assumption of the basic SEFM that the deflection of the springs of
the SEFM are independent of one another. Furthermore, it can be seen that the SEFM
model underestimates the foil deformation for a significant part of the circumference, and
has some discontinuities at the trailing edge of the second pad.

for either model when omitting the point of injection. This is in accordance with the
eccentricity results, and thus supports the assumption that the foil deformation is relevant
to the accuracy of the eccentricity results.
5.5
Discussion
The primary cause of the discrepancy in journal eccentricities between the two one-
dimensional models was assumed to be caused by the assumption of the SEFM model
that the deflection of the springs of the SEFM are independent of one another for three-
pad GFBs. This does not hold true for the four-pad tests, as the pressure is inaccurately
low in most parts of the bearing and exhibs discontinuity at the trailing edge of the second
pad, thereby significantly deteriorating the accuracy of the SEFM model results.
Furthermore, when comparing cases with and without injection, the tendency seen for
three pads that the EB beam element model decreased in accuracy while the non-curved
shell element model increased in accuracy is not seen for four pads. For four pads, no
significant change is seen. One possible explanation for this is the finer discretisation.
Another possible explanation is that the division into four pads results in the injection
through one injector affecting a smaller circumferential area of the foil and thereby having
a less significant influence on the journal eccentricity.
5.6
Conclusion
Similarly to the findings of the article in appendix A, it was found that modelling the top
foil using EB beam elements yielded a satisfactory approximation to that of using a shell
element model with initial curvature, and that the former was a better approximation
to the latter than using a shell element model without initial curvature. Contrary to the
findings in appendix A, the basic SEFM was found to be insufficient both with and without
injection. Lastly, including injection appeared to have little effect on the accuracy of the
EB beam element model and the non-curved shell element model.
Thus, based on the findings in this chapter, it is deemed reasonable to model the top foil
using EB beam elements during the remainder of the project.

6
Stress computations for shell
elements
6.1
Motivation
While investigating top foil modelling methods, it was found that an EB beam approach
yielded better results than a shell element approach with both principal radii initially
infinite, when using a singly curved shell element approach as benchmark, c.f. attached
article in appendix A and chapter 5.
An explanation for the poor performance of the shell element approach with both prin-
cipal radii initially infinite was found in the fact that it significantly overestimates the
dependency of the radial displacement on the axial coordinate. It was theorised that this
was caused by a failure to account for the phenomenon that curvature along one principal
radius of curvature in a thin sheet increases the resistance to curvature along the second
principal radius of curvature, i.e. the presence of significant membrane stresses.
Similar results are seen in the literature. A discrepancy between experimental findings
and a two-dimensional Finite Element (FE) top foil model was found in [35], observing an
overestimation of top foil deflections for the two-dimensional FE as seen both in appendix A
and chapter 5. [35] theorised that this could be caused by the model not taking into account
membrane stresses. In [9], it was observed that membrane stresses are significant for foil
behaviour.
Due to the prevalence of shell element models with both principal radii initially infinite
in the literature within the academic field, and in the industry, it is deemed beneficial to
justify and underline this hypothesis by investigating the membrane and bending stresses
in the two structure types. This has the added benefit of examining whether injection will
compromise the structural integrity of the top foil by causing stresses exceeding the yield
stress of the foil. Thus, the programme was augmented in order to produce these results.
6.2
Theory
The investigated shell elements are curved, isoparametric elements constructed by degen-
erating a solid element by eliminating midside nodes, constraining each pair of nodes on a
thickness-direction line to have the same thickness-direction displacement, and replacing
surface nodes by midsurface nodes with three translational DOF and two rotational DOF
each, as described in [10].
In order to find the membrane and bending components of the stress, the following theo-
retical findings have been used. For a local coordinate system xyz, where z is normal to the
midsurface and x and y are tangent to it, the surface-tangent stresses for a given z-value is
given by a sum of membrane stresses and bending stresses, given by [10] equation (16.1-2):

the bending or twisting moment around the i-axis.

As the shell elements have eight nodes with five DOF per node, the element strain-

The computational procedure for obtaining stresses and components of stresses are carried
out after the solution to the state-space equation has been found. For each bearing, the
corresponding foil states are extracted from the state-space vector. For each element of that
bearing, the DOF numbers, coordinates, direction cosines, and foil states are extracted.
Two functions are called: one that calculates the element stresses and one calculating the
element membrane stresses. Subsequently, these are added to a global stress vector and a
global membrane stress vector respectively. The global bending stress vector can be found
directly by subtraction [10]:

6.3
Case
The case investigated in this chapter corresponds to the one presented in the chapter
on top foil modelling, see section 5.2, as it seeks to further investigate the results of that
section. Thus, the case investigated in this chapter has a constant injector valve clearance,
corresponding to the implementation prior to implementation of variable injector valve
clearance and inclusion of actuator model, see section 4.3.
6.4
Results
Here, the stresses in the top foil of bearing A for the steady state analysis are presented.
Figures with equivalent data for bearing B can be found in appendix E. There exist only
insignificant differences between bearings.
As the shell elements experience a multi-axial state of stress, it is desirable to calculate an
equivalent stress in order to obtain results that can be interpreted with reasonable ease.
An equivalent stress from a multi-axial state can be determined in several ways. While
Von Mises equivalent stress is most often used as a tool for assessing yield, it is one of the
66
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:77==
most widespread ways of calculating an equivalent stress from a multi-axial state of stress
in metals. Other options include using TrescaŌĆÖs equivalent stress, using principal stresses,
or including stresses for all DOF.
In figs. 6.1 and 6.2, it can be seen that the order of magnitude of maximum stresses are
1 ┬Ę 108 Pa, i.e. in the order of 100 MPa.
From [17], it is known that the test rig specific
bump and top foils are heat treated in order to obtain a yield stress exceeding 1 GPa.
It is furthermore noted, that this order of magnitude is necessary, in particular for the
bump foil, in order to avoid the stresses in the foil exceeding the yield strength [17]. Prior
simulation of bump foil also concluded that the maximum stress in the bump foil did not
exceed the yield strength [44]. Similar results were seen for a different case study in [34],
where maximum bump foil stresses had an order of magnitude of 100 MPa. Thus, the
results appear to be in a reasonable order of magnitude.
Most importantly, it can be seen that the membrane stresses for the curved shell element
model is in the order of 1 ┬Ę 107 Pa, i.e. in the order of 10 MPa, whereas they are in the
order of 1 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ7 Pa for the non-curved shell element model. Comparatively, the flexural
stress or the bending stress is in the order of 1 ┬Ę 108 Pa, i.e. in the order of 100 MPa, for
both top foil model implementations.
Furthermore, it can be seen from fig. 6.1 that the maximum sum of stress components
and the flexural stresses predicted by the non-curved element model for the case without
injection is almost twice as large as that predicted by the model with curved elements.
These stresses are located in the part of the top foil that is subject to the overestimated
deformations. Similar tendencies are seen for the case with injection, see fig. 6.2.
Finally, it can be seen that the local stress by injection areas for the present case are
generally less than half of the maximum stresses observed in the top foil. It is seen that
the maximum stresses in the foil is slightly increased by including injection.
For corresponding data for three-pad GFBs, see article in appendix A.
6.5
Discussion
It was observed that the membrane stresses for the curved shell element model were of one
order of magnitude less that the flexural stresses, whereas they were negligible compared to
the flexural stresses for the non-curved shell element model. This supports the hypothesis
presented in chapter 5 that the poor performance of the 2D shell element model is caused
by the overestimation of the dependency on the axial coordinate of the radial deformation
which in turn is caused by the 2D shell element model not accounting for the membrane
stresses caused by the initial curvature of the top foil.
Furthermore, for the case investigated, it was found that local strains caused by injection
did not compromise the top foil by exceeding the yield stress.
While the stress local
to the injection area did not exceed the maximum stress in the foil for the non-injection
implementation, the global maximum stress did increase slightly for the case with injection
compared to the case without injection. However, the stress implementation can be utilised
for control of more critical applications.
6.6
Conclusions
It was found that the tendency of the non-curved shell element model to overestimate
the dependency on the axial coordinate of the radial deformation can be explained by
no significant membrane stresses appearing in the top foil structure as hypothesised in
chapter 5.
