6.7
A note on implementation
This section is primarily for the use of people working on the program in the future.
It should be noted that in order to ease transfer of stress-related code, the C-files have been
collected in a single file called stresses_shells.c with header file stresses_shells.h.
In the header-file, the correct files for functions that belong in other C-files, can be found
in comments. Three additions to the main.c files were necessary:
ŌĆó the preprocessor directive #include "stresses_shell.h" was added to the start
of the program,
ŌĆó a toggle placed in the declarations section: Int calc_stress = 1;, which should
be set to 0 if stresses should not be calculated, and
ŌĆó the following bit of code that calls the main stress-function in the file stresses_shell.c
is inserted after the steady analysis has been carried out and the data has been re-
dimensionalized (and dumped):
1
if (calc_stress == 1) {
2
// calc_stress is a toggle defined in the beginning of main.c, gfbsim.

7

10
}

}
Please note that the code is only executed if the toggle is on, the analysis type is steady,
and the top foil model is a shell element model.
Furthermore, the following changes have been made in python:
ŌĆó an if statement has been added to init_and_run.py that calls the functions to read
the stress into the python code,
ŌĆó the function readfoilmesh has been added to reader.py in order to obtain number
of elements,
ŌĆó the function readstress has been added to reader.py, which includes the calcula-
tion of the Flexural stress vector sigma_flex,
ŌĆó the plotting routine plotstress has been added to plots.py,
ŌĆó the statement from matplotlib import rcParams has been added to plots.py,
ŌĆó the statement import matplotlib.colors has been added to plots.py, and
ŌĆó an if statement containing a call to the plotting routine has been added to init_and_run.py.

==PAGE:81==
7
Discussion
It was found that the basic Simple Elastic Foundation Model exhibited highly inaccurate
journal eccentricities caused by errors and discontinuities in the pressure distribution in
the bearing. While the cause of this has not been identified in the present project, it is
assumed to stem from a problem in the numerical code. However, it is to be expected
that the basic Simple Elastic Foundation Model will be less accurate for four-pad bearings
than for three-pad bearings as the pad edge areas will take up a greater part of the
circumferential area for four-pad bearings, allowing the nonphysical behaviour of the basic
Simple Elastic Foundation Model close to pad edges to have greater influence. The results
for the remaining models in four-pad operation were similar to those seen for three-pad
bearings. It was found that the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element model performed better
than a shell element model with both radii of curvature infinite. It was found that this
was due to the latter overestimating the dependence of the radial deformation on the
axial coordinate. The stresses for the shell element models were investigated and it was
found that the shell element model with both radii of curvature infinite did not generate
membrane stresses. Thus, based on the present work, it is found that the computationally
expensive and implementation-wise complicated model is less accurate than both a singly-
curved shell element model and an EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element model. Furthermore, the
mechanism responsible for this is verified, and it is suggested that one of these modelling
techniques is used in lieu of the non-curved shell element model.
It was seen for three-pad bearings that introducing injection decreased accuracy for the
one-dimensional models. This was not seen for the four-pad bearings. There exists several
possible explanations and further investigation is necessary in order to reach a conclusion.
First, it might be caused by a smaller circumferential area affected by the injection due to
the smaller span of each pad, and the fact that only one injector is open. This could be
investigated by combining the current test with experiments with multiple open injectors
per pad. Alternatively, the effect of injection might be negligible in general. However,
while not found to be extensive, there is seen a clear impact for the three-pad Gas Foil
Bearing. Furthermore, as injection increases the axial variation of foil displacement, it is
expected that the one-dimensional models would become less accurate for hybridisation-
cases. As a result, it appears unlikely that the effect of injection is generally negligible,
and the first explanation is deemed most likely. It would be favourable to carry out more
simulations in order to test the effect of hybridisation on the accuracy of top foil modelling
techniques. Based on the very small discrepancies between the singly-curved shell element
model and the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element model, it is expected that the results will
still be favourable for the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element model. In an industrial context, it
is of great interest to have either the basic Simple Elastic Foundation Model or the EulerŌĆō
Bernoulli beam element foil model available as an alternative to more complex models in
order to reduce computational expenses. Based on the current work, it appears likely that
this is obtainable for the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element model, whereas the basic Simple
Elastic Foundation Model is likely insufficient for hybrid operation.
An actuator model has been successfully added to the existing Gas Foil Bearing simulation
framework. Hereby, a framework for simulation of Hybrid Gas Foil Bearing is prepared
for investigation of the effect on hybridisation on GFB performance and applications.
Furthermore, the foundation for feedback control has been laid. Simulation allows for
investigation into the potential of these technologies within the Gas Foil Bearing field. It
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
71
==PAGE:82==
is expected that hybridisation will significantly increase the usefulness of Gas Foil Bearings
within particularly turbo-machinery.
The actuator model is subject to several limitations. The piezoelectric actuator model
implemented is unable to describe nonlinearities present in the system.
As discussed
previously, it is expected that the actuators used will take this into account. Furthermore,
it should be noted that the present implementation is based on example values for the
injector and is thus not guaranteed to be physical in nature. The inclusion of the actuator
domain increased the simulation times compared to the implementation without injection.
It might prove advantageous to examine whether the current actuator implementation
can be optimised in terms of computational efficiency. However, for the majority of the
runs, the increase in time was approximately 10 %, which is deemed acceptable taking
the significantly more complex test case into account. Likewise, there might be better
ways to handle the problem with input values close to the maximum feeding voltage. This
might deserve investigation. However, the current implementation handles 99.5 % of the
interval spanned by actuator movement. It could likely be increased to 99.75 % without
increasing the maximum input voltage value allowed. As the effect on the fluid film of
smaller injector valve clearances is assumed to be negligible, the programme is deemed
capable of handling most applications of interest in terms of this challenge.
72
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:83==
8
Conclusion
A piezoelectric actuator model was developed analytically and successfully implemented
in the numerical multi-domain code.
The implementation was tested in steady state
operation and yielded equivalent results to the original implementation with air injection,
verifying the implementation. In steady state, there was no difference in computational
expense when comparing the two implementations, while an increase was seen for the
transient implementation. A number of tests and transient simulations were run in order
to test the implementation.
All results were in accordance with the expected system
response, verifying the implementation.
Due to significant discrepancy in the literature, four foil structure modelling techniques
were compared in terms of their accuracy in modelling the compliant structure of both
aerodynamic and hybrid four-pad Gas Foil Bearings.
The results proved independent
of hybridisation. It was found that the basic Simple Elastic Foundation Model did not
provide sufficient accuracy for modelling the foil structure of the four-pad Gas Foil Bear-
ing, although it appeared this was caused by an error in the code. An EulerŌĆōBernoulli
beam element top foil model yielded almost equivalent results to a shell element model
with one radius of curvature infinite and the second equal to the radius of the bearing.
This is in agreement with the findings of appendix A, which analysed both aerodynamic
and hybrid operation, and [35], which analysed only aerodynamic operation. Thus, based
on the findings in the present work, an EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element top foil model is
deemed a viable alternative to more complex and expensive shell element models. This is
of considerable interest due to the resulting significant reduction of computational time,
the comparative simplicity in terms of implementation, as well as the robustness of beam
elements compared to shell elements in terms of element disorders, ill-conditioning etc. A
shell element model with both radii of curvature infinite was likewise tested and found less
accurate than the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element model. In accordance with the findings of
[35], this was found to be caused by the model overestimating the dependence of the axial
coordinate of the radial deformation. Although this can be explained by the phenomenon
that curvature along one principal radius of curvature will give rise to resistance to curva-
ture along the second principal radius of curvature in a shell-like structure, the continual
wide-spread use of the model type in the literature prompted an elaboration on this. As a
result, a stress analysis was carried out, investigating in-plane and out-of-plane stresses for
the shell element models. As predicted, no notable membrane stresses were generated in
the double-infinite shell model, while the singly-curved shell model had membrane stresses
one order of magnitude less than its flexural stresses.
8.1
Future works
The code has been prepared for feedback control. Thus, the determination of a control
scheme and subsequent implementation of a control system into the programme is the most
important continuation of the present work. Depending on the control scheme, the input
vector will become dependent on the state vector and/or the state vector differentiated
with respect to time. Thus, once feedback control is implemented, the Jacobian should
also hold contributions from fG,u. Where these should be included depends on the control
scheme. It would be advantageous to carry out the implementation in such a way that
the programme can handle all possible variations of the input signal dependencies, and
automatically models the Jacobians based on the given input-function.
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
73
==PAGE:84==
System parameters for the injector should be determined. Furthermore, for more widespread
or commercial use, a methodology for the determination of these parameters could be in-
vestigated.
As discussed in the thesis, the modified supply pressure will be unknown and not equal
to the supply pressure in a given test rig. It is suggested that this is analysed by creating
a CFD model that includes the supply pressure inlet. Another course of action would
be to investigate whether the two pressures are related through a correction factor or a
mathematical expression that can be determined through experiments or simulation.
It is suggested that the effect of hysteresis nonlinearities and other nonlinearities could be
investigated and if found significant, included in the numerical framework.
Furthermore, it might be of interest to optimise the efficiency of the actuator implemen-
tation as well as investigating a better way to handle problems with input values close to
the voltage maximum value.
It is recommended that simulations with several open injectors are carried out in order
to test the different top foil modelling techniques. As part of this, it is suggested that
the problem experienced with the basic Simple Elastic Foundation Model is investigated
further.

Article: Numerical modelling of

Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
79
==PAGE:90==
Numerical modelling of compliant foil structure in gas foil bearings: Com-
parison of four top foil models with and without radial injection
Signe T. Heinemann1, Sebastian von Osmanski2, Ilmar F. Santos3
1 Department of Mechanical Engineering, Technical University of Denmark, 2800 Kgs.
Lyngby, Denmark,
s147533@student.dtu.dk
2 Department of Mechanical Engineering, Technical University of Denmark, 2800 Kgs.
Lyngby, Denmark,
asvosm@mek.dtu.dk
3 Department of Mechanical Engineering, Technical University of Denmark, 2800 Kgs.
Lyngby, Denmark,
ifs@mek.dtu.dk
Abstract
In the literature, there exists no consensus on a best practice for modelling the compliant foil structure of Gas
Foil Bearings (GFBs). This paper focuses on the top foil modelling and its original contribution to the modelling
problem is a comparison of steady state analysis ef’¼üciency and accuracy between four top foil models with and
without gas injection. The bump foil is modelled using the Simple Elastic Foundation Model (SEFM), and the
four top foil models are a) neglecting the top foil, i.e. the foil structure is modelled as a basic SEFM, b) EulerŌĆō
Bernoulli beam elements, c) non-curved shell elements, and d) curved shell elements. The theoretical analysis
is carried out using a multi-domain numerical model and exempli’¼üed using a rigid rotor supported by three-pad
GFBs. Comparing steady state journal eccentricities, low discrepancies are seen between almost all investigated
models. However, the basic SEFM is found to be insuf’¼ücient for cases with injection. Using the curved model
as a benchmark, it is found that the predictions of steady state journal eccentricities made by the model with
non-curved shell elements are less accurate than those of the model with EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements. Thus,
despite a higher degree of simpli’¼ücation, the model with EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements is found to be both more
computationally ef’¼ücient as well as more accurate for steady state analysis when compared to the model with non-
curved shell elements. While the radial foil deformation for the curved model exhibits dependency on the axial
coordinate, this dependency is signi’¼ücantly overestimated by the model using non-curved shell elements. This is
most pronounced for the case without injection, and it could explain the inferiority of the model using non-curved
shell elements for estimating steady state journal eccentricities. A stress analysis reveal that this is caused by the
non-curved shell element model not accounting for the membrane stresses in the top foil.
Key words: Gas foil bearings, Numerical modelling, Steady state analysis, Compliant foil structure, Radial gas
injection, Computational complexity, Membrane stresses

==PAGE:92==
1
Introduction
Over the past decades, signi’¼ücant research advancements [1, 9, 13, 14, 18, 22] have allowed Gas Foil Bear-
ings (GFBs) to become important for a range of applications including air-cycle machines [1, 1, 16, 18], computer
disk drives [6], machines in clean environments [1, 9, 17], and high-speed machinery [1, 9, 16, 16, 17]. GFBs
are a type of ’¼éuid bearings using a gas as lubricant to separate the rotor from a compliant foil structure. Several
distinct types of foil con’¼ügurations comprising the compliant bearing structure have been developed and employed
[1, 9, 18]. The most common of these and the one under investigation in the present paper is the bump-type foil
con’¼üguration [1]. The appeal of GFBs rests on some of their key advantages. As air can be used as a lubricant, the
bearing type can reduce or eliminate working ’¼éuid contamination [9, 17], and the bearing type has potential as an
environmentally friendly machine element [10]. Furthermore, the abundance of air in most operating environments
of rotating machinery eliminates the need for a lubrication system as long as the bearing is purely aerodynamic
[1, 9, 16], without necessitating back-up bearings and control systems as required by Active Magnetic Bearings
(AMBs) [3]. The comparatively low viscosity of gases likewise reduces energy losses [18]. GFBs have no contact
between bearing and journal when the machine is running, resulting in negligible wear occurring at operating ve-
locities [2]. GFBs are furthermore well-suited for noise-sensitive applications, e.g. in computers and high-speed
machinery [16]. Another reason that GFBs are advantageous for these applications is that they have signi’¼ücantly
lower temperature sensitivity [1, 16] than e.g. oil lubricated bearings [17]. Some characteristics of GFBs limit
their use at the present time. As currently employed GFBs are purely aerodynamic, a high velocity is necessary
to generate a consistent ’¼éuid ’¼ülm separating bearing and journal, why signi’¼ücant wear occurs at start and stop
[2, 9, 17]. Current GFBs are well-suited only for light rotors and relatively high velocities due to the low viscosity
of the lubricant and they require ’¼üne tolerances in production and high precision in assembly [9, 17, 18]. The low
viscosity of the lubricant results in low damping [9]. In combination with an inherently cross-coupled stiffness
[9, 17] and sub-synchronous vibrations [18], this challenges the dynamical stability.
One approach to improving these limiting characteristics of GFBs is hybridisation using radial injection of
pressurised gas. Similar approaches are used in rigid gas bearings as well as static and hybrid oil journal bearings.
Recent ’¼ündings suggest a potential improvement of load capacity, stability limits, as well as wear and operation
at low velocities [24]. While hybridisation can improve performance and allow implementation of GFBs in ad-
ditional applications, the advantages should be weighed against the complexity, weight, and space requirement
added by the injection system. One ’¼üeld where hybridisation has particular relevance is heavy high-speed machin-
ery. Whether injection is used or not, it is necessary to accurately model system behaviour, which is complicated
by the multi-domain nature of the problem resulting in scaling issues, the high compressibility of the lubricant,
and the compliant foil which, amongst other things, inhibits use of perturbation methods [14].
The present paper focuses on methods for modelling the top foil. As the methods are of varying computational
complexity and their comparative precision is unknown, no current consensus on best practice exists. As the prob-
lems are inherently computationally complex and as a high precision is crucial for system design and diagnostics,
charting these parameters as well as obtaining a satisfactory compromise between precision and computational
complexity is vital. While this is important for steady state analysis, it is fundamental for dynamic analysis due to
the increased computational complexity. Most of the commonly employed models for the compliant foil structure
uses a version of the Simple Elastic Foundation Model (SEFM) originally presented in 1983 [7] for the bump foil.
Various modi’¼ücations have been proposed [11, 22], including the commonly applied version that incorporates a
structural loss factor ╬Ę, used e.g. in [8, 12, 13, 23]. When modelling the entire compliant structure with a basic
SEFM, only the bump foil is accounted for, neglecting the effect of the top foil. A different approach combines
the SEFM with a top foil modelled by EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements, used e.g. in [20, 24]. A third approach
exchanges the one-dimensional top foil elements for plate elements, used e.g. in [20]. It should be noted that it has
been found by [4] that membrane stresses are signi’¼ücant for foil behaviour. As membrane forces are not accounted
for by plate bending theory, this indicates that plate elements are insuf’¼ücient for modelling the top foil. As a fourth
approach, an SEFM can be used in combination with a top foil model consisting of curved shell elements, used e.g.
in [18]. Other approaches include a Finite Element (FE) model of the bump foil structure [15], a top foil model
using EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements combined with a truss-based bump foil model [22], and using an FE bump
foil model in combination with a top foil model using two-dimensional plate linear elements [11].
The main original contribution of this paper is a comparison of the precision and accuracy of a steady state
analysis and foil deformation of four top foil models with and without radial injection of gas. The bump foil is
modelled using the SEFM throughout. The four top foil models are a) neglecting the top foil corresponding to using
a basic SEFM, b) EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements, c) non-curved shell elements, and d) curved shell elements.
3

Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:93==
2
Modelling
The rotor is modelled as rigid and supported by two three-pad GFBs, see ’¼üg. 1 for a schematic of the shaftŌĆō
bearing con’¼üguration as well as the GFBs. The theoretical analysis is carried out using a multi-domain numerical
model coupling the rotor, foil, and ’¼éuid domains. A generic framework for the model is detailed in [21], and the
incorporation of injection into this framework can be found in [24]. The convergence of the coupled model is
equivalent to that of the outer solver of perturbation methods that are commonly used, and as these also include an
inner solver, the coupled model has the potential to signi’¼ücantly reduce computational time [23]. In the following
subsections, the fundamental assumptions underlying the model are presented.
2.1
Fluid ’¼ülm
The ’¼éuid ’¼ülm is modelled as detailed in [23] and summarised in [24]. In the present paper, fundamental
assumptions are outlined. Assuming that the working gas is isothermal, isoviscous, and ideal, the ’¼éuid ’¼ülm is
modelled using the Modi’¼üed Reynolds Equation (MRE) discretised using a non-uniform structured Cartesian grid,
see ’¼üg. 2, and a Finite Volume (FV) approach. The non-uniform grid is used in order to capture the steep pressure
gradients close to the injection areas. For the case without injection, the non-uniform grid is retained in order to
allow comparison between cases. It is assumed that the pressure at the leading and trailing edges of the pads are
equal to the ambient pressure as well as constant with respect to the axial direction at either end of the bearing. The
rotor angular velocity Žē is used as the characteristic frequency ŽēŽä [24]. A Central Differencing Scheme (CDS)
is used for determining pressure gradients from Control Volume Centre (CVC) pressures, and a Linear Upwind
Differencing Scheme (LUDS) is used for estimating pressure values.
2.2
Injection
A framework for non-controlled injection of pressurised air using a ’¼éow restriction control strategy by control-
ling the injector valve clearance hinj, see ’¼üg. 3, is described in [24]. The fundamental principle will be outlined
here. Based on information about the injector geometry and a systematic variation of key parameters, a parameter
map is built using Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) results. This parameter map is used for determining
injected mass ’¼éow from a set of parameters which the mass ’¼éow is sensitive to: nozzle clearance, injection pres-
sure, ’¼éuid ’¼ülm pressure, journal eccentricity, and rotor angular velocity [24]. This pre-generated parameter map is
subsequently used to incorporate injection through interpolation into the coupled model initially presented in [21],
obtaining a model of a Hybrid Gas Foil Bearing (HGFB) with non-controlled radial injection.
The parameter map used in the present paper is developed by von Osmanski [24] using the CFD programme
OpenFoam v1906 for creating a sub-model of the injector connected to a small part of the ’¼éuid ’¼ülm. The narrowest
cross sections in the model are discretised using eight Control Volumes (CVs), resulting in a total sub-model size
of 212400 CVs [24]. In the current paper, the injection ’¼éow is assumed laminar and steady, all walls are assumed

2.3
Rotor
The rotor model is detailed in [24], and constitutes a rigid rotor represented by two Degrees of Freedom (DOFs)
at either of the two bearing locations, that is:
╦£xr = {╬ĄAx
╬ĄAy
╬ĄBx
╬ĄBy}T
(1)
where ╦£xr is the non-dimensionalised rotor DOF vector, ╬Ąix and ╬Ąiy are the x- and y-directional journal eccentrici-
ties, and i = A, B signify the two bearing positions.
2.4
Gas Foil Bearing
Four different foil structure con’¼ügurations are investigated. It is assumed that the top foil is unable to separate
from the bump foil, and thus, the G┬©umbel condition is used during the dynamic pressure integration [23].
The bump foil contributes to both stiffness and damping matrix of the foil structure and is modelled as an
SEFM with stiffness k and modi’¼üed to include a loss factor ╬Ę [24]. The foil oscillation frequency in’¼éuences the
effective damping contribution for the SEFM [24].
The simplest foil structure model investigated neglects the top foil, i.e. assumes that the top foil has no struc-
tural stiffness or mass, and models the entire foil structure using the basic SEFM modi’¼üed using the loss factor.
The second investigated top foil model contributes to the foil structure stiffness matrix by modelling the top foil
as EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements with two nodes per element. Each node has one translational and one rotational
DOF, and the ’¼ürst node of each pad is constrained. A detailed description can be found in [24]. For the two
remaining models investigated in the present paper, eight-node curved isoparametric shell elements as detailed in
[5] are used. Four of the eight nodes are corner-nodes and four are mid-side nodes. All nodes have three transla-
tional and two rotational DOFs. The leading edge nodes of each pad are constrained corresponding to a clamped
con’¼üguration, and all axial DOFs are constrained. A symmetry condition is used. While the element formulation
for both models are equivalent, the two models differ by the initial curvature of the shell elements. For one model,
the shell elements are curved corresponding to their placement around the circumference of the bearing. Thus,
these elements are singly curved shell elements where the ’¼ünite principal radius corresponds to the distance to the
bearing centre. For the other model, the shell elements are initially laid out in two-dimensional space, i.e. the foil
is unfolded in the circumferential direction thereby having two in’¼ünite principal radii. It should be emphasised that
a signi’¼ücant difference between the one-dimensional models and the shell elements is that the latter determines an
axially dependent foil de’¼éection on the axially dependent pressure, whereas the one-dimensional models assume
axially independent foil de’¼éection and use an axially averaged pressure.
5
84
Design of controllable segmented foil bearings based on multi-physics modelling techniques
==PAGE:95==
2.5
Coupled model
The foil and rotor are cast in state-space residual form, and all domains are combined into a global non-linear
system of equations as described in [23]. The injection contributions are included in the FV equations for the ’¼éuid
’¼ülm pressure [24].
3
Cases
The comparison of the four top foil models are carried out for an example case with and without injection
outlined in this section. The rotor is supported by two three-pad GFBs. Case data can be found in table 1. Note
that the Walowit foil stiffness is found using the foil YoungŌĆÖs modulus, bump half length, bump foil pitch, bump
foil thickness, and PoissonŌĆÖs ratio. For all models except the basic SEFM without a top foil model, 10 beam
or shell elements are used per pad in the circumferential direction, corresponding to 30 elements for the entire
circumference. For the models with shell elements, 6 elements are used in the axial direction. The ’¼éuid ’¼ülm
mesh for one pad can be seen in ’¼üg. 2. For the case without injection, the steady state analysis is simulated under
aerodynamic operation with air as lubricant. All three injectors are closed. For the case with injection, the top-
most injector is open with injector valve clearance hinj = 35 ┬Ąm and injection pressure pinj = 400 kPa, while the
remaining two injectors are closed. For the case with injection, a parameter-map approach is used as described
in section 2.2. The parameter map and the injector geometry used are calculated and presented in the article [24]
using the parameters and levels seen in table 2.

and the top foil modelling technique are varied, the latter as described in section 2.4. The foil model employing
curved shell elements is used as a benchmark as it is the least simpli’¼üed, and thus expected to be the most accu-

analysis. As the background activity of the computer was not fully controllable, the in’¼éuence of this was sought
minimised by analysing the results of ’¼üfty runs for each case. The data was analysed for potential outliers and
distribution. The mean and median of the runs are given in table 4. As the data did not appear normally distributed,
the standard deviation is not given. Furthermore, the median is used as the measure of computational time as it is
less sensitive to outliers.

SEFM requires approximately 25 % of the time spent by the curved shell element model, while the non-curved
shell element model requires 80 % more time than the curved shell element model. As the system matrix for the
non-curved shell element model is less dense than that of the curved shell element model, this is most likely caused
by Reynolds Equation (RE) converging signi’¼ücantly slower for large foil deformations. For the passive case, an
insigni’¼ücant decrease in required computational time is seen for most cases. However, for the basic SEFM without
injection, an increase in computational time to approximately 40 % of the time required by the curved shell element
model is seen. It was found that the residual vector, speci’¼ücally for the ’¼éuid domain, relevant to the convergence
criteria was one order of magnitude smaller for this speci’¼üc case compared to the remaining seven cases. Thus, it is
argued that this estimate is not comparable to the remaining results, as it meets a signi’¼ücantly higher convergence
criteria.
A possible explanation for the discrepancies in journal eccentricities is found by examining the foil deformation
estimated by the four models.
4.1
Foil deformations for case without injection
In this section, the bearing A foil deformations for the steady state analysis without injection are presented.
Figures containing equivalent data for bearing B can be found in appendix A. No signi’¼ücant differences are ob-
served between bearings.
In ’¼üg. 5, the one-dimensional models are plotted against the axial average of the shell element models. It can be
seen that all four models yield almost equivalent approximations of the axial average of the radial foil deformation.
The estimated foil deformation for the basic SEFM and the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam model can be seen in ’¼üg. 6a
and ’¼üg. 6b respectively. Comparing the radial foil deformation of the basic SEFM and the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam
model, the most notable difference is the zero displacement of the trailing edges of the pads for the basic SEFM.
This is caused by the assumption of the basic SEFM that the de’¼éection of the springs of the SEFM are independent
of one another. This assumption is expected to be the primary cause of the discrepancy in journal eccentricities
between the two models.
The radial foil deformation of the models with shell elements (cases 3 and 4) can likewise be seen in ’¼üg. 6. For
the model with curved shell elements, cf. ’¼ügs. 6c and 6e, it can be seen that the largest axial variations in radial
deformation are approximately from 5.9 ┬Ąm to 7.3 ┬Ąm, or equivalently, from 2.1 ┬Ąm to 3.3 ┬Ąm. For the model with
Figure 5: Comparison of average radial deformation of bearing A for the four foil models without injection. Cases

==PAGE:100==
non-curved shell elements, cf. ’¼ügs. 6d and 6f, the largest axial variations are approximately from 2.9 ┬Ąm to 7.8 ┬Ąm,
and, equivalently, from 0.5 ┬Ąm to 3.8 ┬Ąm. Furthermore, the sub-zero radial deformations vary signi’¼ücantly more
for the model with non-curved shell elements compared to the model with curved shell elements.
The discrepancies of the journal eccentricities between the two shell models are assumed to be caused by this
exaggeration of the axial dependency of the radial foil deformation of the non-curved model. The exaggeration is
expected to be caused by a failure to account for increased stiffness due to initial curvature. Physical thin sheets
exhibit dependency between longitudinal and transverse curvature [19]. Shell elements, unlike plate elements,
account for this. Thus, by neglecting initial curvature, the non-curved shell element model does not take into
account that curvature along one principal radius of curvature increases the resistance to curvature along the second
principal radius of curvature, i.e. that it has a stiffening effect in the direction of the second principal radius of
curvature.
The discrepancies between the model with EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements and the model with curved shell
elements are assumed to be caused by the assumption of uniform axial de’¼éection for the former.
4.2
Foil deformations for case with injection
In this section, the bearing A foil deformations for the steady state analysis with injection are presented. Fig-
ures containing equivalent data for bearing B can be found in appendix A. No signi’¼ücant differences are observed
between bearings.
In ’¼üg. 7, the one-dimensional models are plotted against the axial average of the shell element models. It can be
seen that all four models yield almost equivalent approximations of the axial average of the radial foil deformation,
although the independence of each spring in the SEFM appears to cause a signi’¼ücant discrepancy at the trailing
edge of the ’¼ürst pad, and at the point of injection, i.e. at the midpoint of the third pad, see also ’¼üg. 8a versus ’¼üg. 8b.
The radial foil deformation of the models with shell elements (cases 3 and 4) can likewise be seen in ’¼üg. 8. For
the model with curved shell elements, cf. ’¼ügs. 8c and 8e, it can be seen that the largest axial variations in radial
deformation are approximately from 6.8 ┬Ąm to 8.4 ┬Ąm, from 2.6 ┬Ąm to 4.3 ┬Ąm, or from 0.8 ┬Ąm to 1.9 ┬Ąm.
For the model with non-curved shell elements, cf. ’¼ügs. 8d and 8f, the largest axial variations are approximately
from 3.9 ┬Ąm to 8.8 ┬Ąm, from 0.7 ┬Ąm to 4.9 ┬Ąm, or from ŌłÆ0.01 ┬Ąm to 3.0 ┬Ąm. Furthermore, the sub-zero radial
deformations vary signi’¼ücantly more for the model with non-curved shell elements compared to the model with
curved shell elements.

==PAGE:102==
The discrepancies of the journal eccentricities between the two shell models are assumed to be caused by this
exaggeration of the axial dependency of the radial foil deformation of the non-curved model. Comparing cases, i.e.
with and without injection, it can be seen that the general tendency is that including injection will cause the axial
dependency to increase for the curved model and decrease for the non-curved model. As this is in accordance with
the eccentricity results, this supports the assumption that the foil deformation is related to the accuracy of these
results. However, a signi’¼ücant increase in axial dependency is seen for the third pad with injection and without
curvature due to the deformation at the bearing edge being almost zero. A second exception is the second pad for
the curved model that is virtually unchanged with and without injection. The discrepancies between the model
with EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements and the model with curved shell elements are still assumed to be caused by
the assumption of uniform axial de’¼éection for the former.
4.3
Stress components
In this section, the bearing A stress components for the steady state analysis with and without injection are
presented. Figures containing equivalent data for bearing B can be found in appendix B. No signi’¼ücant differences
are observed between bearings.
As can be seen from ’¼üg. 9 and ’¼üg. 10, the maximum ’¼éexural stresse are overestimated by the non-curved shell
element model. This overestimation is linked to the areas subject to large axial variations of radial de’¼éection. It
is also seen that the membrane stresses for the non-curved shell element model is in the order of 1 ├Ś 10ŌłÆ7 Pa,
whereas they are in the order of 10 MPa for the curved shell element model. In comparison, the ’¼éexural stresses
are in the order of 100 MPa for both models. Thus, as the in-plane stresses in the top foil of the non-curved shell
element model are negligible, this af’¼ürms the hypothesis that the discrepancies in top foil de’¼éections seen between
the two shell models are caused by the non-curved shell element model not taking into account the increase of
resistance to curvature caused by the initial curvature of the foil.
5
Conclusions and future aspects
Comparing steady state journal eccentricities for the four top foil models without injection yielded one-
directional deviations below 2.5 % and a maximum distance between any two models of 0.008 of the radial
clearance, signifying that all four investigated models have reasonable accuracy for eccentricities. This maxi-
mum deviation from and distance to the benchmark case with curved shell elements was seen for the basic SEFM,
i.e. by neglecting the top foil. Improved accuracy is seen for non-curved shell elements supported by an SEFM.
Without injection, the Euclidean distance was approximately 1.7 times that of the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element
model, while the maximum one-directional deviation was 1.6 %. Of the investigated models, EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam
elements supported by an SEFM best approximated the results of a model with curved shell elements. Without
injection, the Euclidean distance to the benchmark case was 40 % of that of the basic SEFM, and the maximum
one-directional deviation was 1.2 %. For included injection, the best performance is likewise seen for the model
with EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam elements and the worst performance for the basic SEFM. The former sees a slight
increase in Euclidean distance and a maximum one-directional deviation of 1.7 %. The non-curved shell element
model improves by including injection with Euclidean distance approximately 1.3 times that of the EulerŌĆōBernoulli
beam element model, while the maximum one-directional deviation was 1.4 %. Including injection signi’¼ücantly
deteriorated the accuracy of the basic SEFM with one-directional deviations of approximately 7 % and the distance
to the curved shell element model of approximately 0.017 of the radial clearance.
With injection, the one-dimensional models have comparable computational times approximately one fourth
of the curved shell model. The non-curved shell model requires almost twice as much time as the curved shell
element model. Without injection, similar results are seen.
A possible explanation for the accuracy discrepancies is presented by evaluating the effect of top foil model
on foil structure radial deformation. Using a top foil model with curved shell elements, it was seen that the radial
deformation exhibits dependency on the axial coordinate, but that this dependency is signi’¼ücantly overestimated
by using non-curved shell elements. This overestimation is more signi’¼ücant for the case without injection than for
the case with injection. Based on a stress analysis, it was found that this overestimation was linked to a failure to
account for membrane stresses by the non-curved shell element model.
It was found that all four models estimated approximately equivalent foil deformations averaged with respect
to the axial coordinate. Furthermore, as the dependency for the model with curved shell elements is relatively small
and based on the steady state journal eccentricities found by the four models, it is argued that the approximation of
constant radial deformation in the axial direction is reasonable for steady state analysis even when including injec-
tion. However, the basic SEFM model appears to be insuf’¼ücient for describing system behaviour when injection is

introduced.
The favourable performance of the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element model is in agreement with the results of
[20], where it was found that a one-dimensional beam-like top foil exhibited satisfactory compliance with exper-
imentally obtained ’¼ülm thickness data while a plate element structure overestimated the minimum ’¼ülm thickness
at the bearing centre-line while underestimating it at the bearing edges. One of the most important ’¼ündings of this
paper is that the same tendency is seen when including injection and that including injection does not signi’¼ücantly
deteriorate the steady state analysis performance of the EulerŌĆōBernoulli beam element model. This is of signi’¼ü-
cant relevance for simulation of GFBs. The present paper treats steady state analysis. The results are of greater
interest for the dynamic analysis of the system. The signi’¼ücant differences in computational time observed for the
steady state analysis will be of importance for these computationally more complex analyses, and the potential for
reduction of computational time without signi’¼ücant loss of accuracy for an EulerŌĆōBernoulli top foil model is thus
of particular interest.

without injection. Cases 1 (basic SEFM) and 2 (EB beam elements) are represented by

elements) and 4 (curved shell elements) are represented by their axial averages.

with injection. Cases 1 (basic SEFM) and 2 (EB beam elements) are represented by linear
interpolated values and shape functions respectively. Cases 3 (non-curved shell elements)
and 4 (curved shell elements) are represented by their axial averages.
